## **CHAPTER 1**

The rain fell like a judgment on the capital, a warm, heavy rain that turned the red earth to blood and the new tarmac to a slick, black mirror. It drummed a funeral march on the roof of the black Mercedes 600 Pullman, a sound that drowned out the low, anxious muttering of the city. Inside the car, the air was cold and smelled of imported leather and gun oil. Colonel Abayomi “The Leopard” Adeshina did not look at the rain. He watched the reflections of the streetlights warp and slide across the wet window, each one a dying sun.

“They are calling it ‘The Midnight Sun,’ sir,” said Captain Ikenna from the front passenger seat, his voice tight. He held a transistor radio to his ear, the tinny voice of the state broadcaster leaking out. “A new dawn of prosperity. A testament to your vision.”

Adeshina said nothing. Vision was a currency he had minted himself, and it was now so devalued it bought nothing but the hollow echo of his own voice. The car slowed, its tires hissing. Outside, the monolithic silhouette of the People’s Unity Tower—his Tower—pierced the low belly of the clouds, its apex lost in the weather and the dark. It was lit from below by banks of floodlights, a stark, brutalist needle stitched into the flesh of the night. At its peak, a rotating beacon, his personal addition, swept the sky. The Midnight Sun. It was meant to be a symbol of vigilance, a light that never set on the revolution. Tonight, it looked like a searchlight hunting for escapees.

“The delegation from the World Bank has been seated in the Grand Ballroom,” Ikenna continued, putting the radio down. “The French ambassador is… appreciative of the new mining concessions. The Soviet advisor asked again about the missile silos.”

“Let him ask,” Adeshina murmured, his first words in twenty minutes. His voice was a dry rustle, like pages turning in a forgotten ledger. “He asks. We listen. We do not answer.”

The car passed through the final checkpoint, a gauntlet of young soldiers in dripping ponchos, their faces rigid with a fear that had nothing to do with the rain. Their salutes were so sharp they seemed to cut the air. Adeshina did not acknowledge them. To acknowledge was to see the individual, and individuals were a currency of diminishing returns. He saw only a system, a machine he had built, its gears now grinding with a sand he could not locate.

The Pullman glided into the vast, marbled undercroft of the Tower. The rain was abruptly silenced, replaced by the echo of the engine and the soft, efficient clicks of heels on stone. His door was opened not by Ikenna, but by a tall, severe woman in the crisp grey uniform of the State Security Directorate. Major Funmilayo Ransom. Her hair was shorn close to her skull, her eyes the colour of old flint.

“Colonel.” Her voice was cool, devoid of the sycophantic warmth of the others. “A matter requires your attention. Before the ceremony.”

He stepped out, adjusting the fit of his dress uniform tunic, a masterpiece of midnight-blue serge and braided gold that felt heavier than armour. “If it required my attention, Major, it would have found me already.”

“It has been waiting,” she said, falling into step beside him as he moved towards his private elevator. Ikenna trailed behind, a nervous shadow. “In the sub-level archive. Section Seven.”

Adeshina stopped. The elevator doors, polished to a liquid bronze, reflected a fractured version of his face—the strong jaw gone granite-hard, the famous scar along his temple a pale river through his close-cropped hair. Section Seven was not a place. It was a euphemism. It was where the past was stored, not catalogued, but interred.

“What is in Section Seven that breathes?” he asked.

“Not what,” Ransom said, meeting his reflection’s eyes. “Who. The architect.”

The word hung in the chilled air. The architect. There was only one man who bore that title in Adeshina’s mind. Kwame Asare. The Ghanaian poet-intellectual who had drawn the first sketches of this new society on napkins in a Paris café, twenty-five years ago. The dreamer. The man whose vision Adeshina had forged into steel and concrete and law. The man he had quietly, mercifully, consigned to the dustbin of history a decade past. A necessary excision. Poets make poor engineers of state.

“Asare is in Switzerland,” Adeshina stated, a flat fact. “He teaches. He writes his obscure verse. He is retired.”

“He was in Switzerland,” Ransom corrected, her voice low. “He crossed the border at Togo four days ago. A fishing pirogue. Our border security is a sieve, as you know. He arrived in the capital this morning. He asked to see you. He was… detained for questioning.”

Adeshina felt a cold, familiar anger, but beneath it, something else—a quick, silvery flicker of unease, like a fish seen in deep water. Kwame Asare. Here. Now, on the night of his ultimate triumph, the unveiling of the Tower that was the physical manifestation of their—*his*—dream.

“Why bring him here?” Adeshina’s question was a blade.

“He insisted. He said he had a gift for the Midnight Sun. A foundational document.” Ransom’s expression did not change. “He said you would understand. He said it was ‘the original covenant.’”

The air in the garage seemed to grow thinner. Adeshina could hear the distant, muffled thump of the state symphony orchestra tuning up in the ballroom sixty floors above. A fanfare of oboes and kora. A celebration of light. And below, in the dark, a ghost with a covenant.

“Bring him to my viewing chamber,” Adeshina said, stepping into the elevator. “Not the archive. I will see him alone.”

“Sir, protocol—” Ikenna began.

“Protocol is the ritual we perform to forget we are animals, Captain,” Adeshina said, the elevator doors beginning to close on their worried faces. “Tonight, I wish to remember.”

The elevator did not go up. It descended. It was a small, silent box, panelled in a rare, dark mahogany. It smelled of beeswax and isolation. He had built a vertical nation, and he had reserved its deepest root for himself. The doors opened onto a circular chamber, not large, with one curved wall of impenetrable black glass overlooking the city. The other walls were bare, poured concrete. In the centre was a single, backless chair of polished steel. A throne for a watchman.

He did not sit. He stood before the window, watching the spectral glow of his city through the rain. The planned sectors, geometric and clean. The old quarters, a tangled darkness where the lights were sporadic. The wide boulevards he had carved through the heart of the old capital, now rivers of reflected neon. His masterpiece. And yet, from this vantage, it looked less like a city and more like a complex circuit board, waiting for a current that would either bring it to life or fry it to ash.

The door hissed open. He did not turn.

The footsteps were slow, shuffling. A dry cough. Then a voice, once rich and melodic, now cracked with age and something else.

“Abayomi. You have built a magnificent cage.”

Adeshina turned. The man in the doorway was a shrunken monument. Kwame Asare stood in a simple, mud-stained *boubou*, leaning on a cane of twisted *nsaa* wood. His hair was a shock of white wool. His face, once so open and fervent, was a map of deep lines and sorrows. But his eyes, behind smudged spectacles, held the same unsettling clarity, the same ability to look at a thing and see not its form, but its shadow.

“You look well, Kwame,” Adeshina said, the old lie coming easily. “Swiss air agrees with you.”

“It is clean. It is quiet. It smells of money and neutrality. It is a good place for a ghost to wait.” Asare took a few painful steps into the room, his gaze sweeping the concrete, the steel, the monolithic window. “This room… it is the mind of the state, is it not? All observation. No vulnerability. Very you.”

“You did not come to critique my interior design.”

“No.” Asare stopped a few feet away. He smelled of dust, of long travel, of cheap soap. “I came to give you your gift. And to ask you a question.”

From within the folds of his *boubou*, Asare drew out not a weapon, not a document, but a simple, worn leather notebook, its cover stained by time and touch. He held it out. It was an intimate, terrifying gesture.

“The covenant,” Asare said.

Adeshina did not take it. He knew that book. He had watched Asare fill it, years ago, in the heat of the struggle. Poems, manifestos, sketches of a just society. The sacred text of their youth.

“We have a constitution now, Kwame. Thicker. With more lawyers.”

“This is not law. This is memory.” Asare’s arm did not waver. “This is the seed. I have come to see the tree. And I find it… magnificent. And strangling everything that once grew beneath it.”

The cold anger returned, solid and useful. “You speak of strangling? You, who fled at the first touch of hardship? Who chose exile over the hard, bloody work of building? You left the blueprint and ran from the construction site. Do not now critique the plumbing.”

A sad smile touched Asare’s cracked lips. “I did not run from the work, Abayomi. I ran from the worker. I saw the blueprint becoming a blueprint for something else. I saw the dream in your eyes hardening into a scheme.” He tapped the notebook with a bony finger. “It is all in here. What we promised. What we swore to the people in the dark. To the spirits of the land. To each other.”

“The people have light now!” Adeshina’s voice, usually so controlled, cracked like a whip in the sterile room. He gestured violently at the window, at the glittering, rain-blurred city. “They have roads. Schools. A standing in the world that does not begin with a beggar’s bowl! You wanted a nation that could look the world in the eye. Well, its eyes are open, Kwame. And they are not blinking.”

“And what does it see?” Asare whispered. “When it looks in the mirror, what does it see? A face, or a mask?”

The silence that followed was total, broken only by the almost imperceptible hum of the Tower’s vast machinery. Adeshina stared at the old man, at the offered book. He saw not a poet, but the first crack in the foundation. A single, hairline fracture. To ignore it was to let the damp in. To acknowledge it was to admit the foundation could be flawed.

He took the notebook. The leather was shockingly warm.

“My question,” Asare said, his voice gaining a sliver of its old strength. “The one I travelled here to ask. The one that keeps a ghost from resting.”

Adeshina waited, the book heavy in his hand.

“Is there anything left of the man who swore those oaths with me?” Asare asked. “Or did I help build a palace for a new, different king? One who wears the same face, but sold the heart to pay for the stones?”

Adeshina did not answer. He walked to the steel chair and sat, placing the notebook on his lap. He looked from the ghost of his past to the glittering testament of his present.

“You will be my guest tonight, Kwame,” he said, his voice once more the dry, administrative rustle. “You will see the Midnight Sun lit. You will see the world applaud. Then, you will return to your Swiss quiet. This…” he lifted the notebook slightly, “…will remain with me. A historical curiosity.”

Asare’s shoulders slumped, not in defeat, but in a profound, weary confirmation. “You did not answer.”

“The ceremony begins in one hour,” Adeshina said, pressing a button on the chair’s arm. The door hissed open. Major Ransom stood there, a silent sentinel. “Major, escort our… honoured guest to a viewing suite. See that he is comfortable.”

Asare turned, leaning heavily on his cane. At the door, he looked back, his eyes finding Adeshina’s in the dark glass. “You should read it, Abayomi. Start with the poem about the baobab. The one we wrote the night they killed Ekon.” He paused. “The tree remembers the seed, even when the man forgets.”

He shuffled out, followed by Ransom. The door sealed shut.

Alone, Adeshina sat in the silence of his vertical kingdom. He looked down at the notebook in his hands. He did not open it. He placed it on the cold concrete floor beside his chair.

Above him, sixty stories of power, celebration, and light. Below him, the dark earth, and the ghosts it held.

The rain beat against the black glass, a thousand tiny, insistent fists.

## **CHAPTER 2**

The viewing suite was a gilded cage on the fifty-fifth floor, all plush velvet and cold chrome, with a wall of glass offering a soaring, nauseating view of the capital. They had given Kwame Asare a stiff-backed chair, a carafe of water, and a young State Security officer who stood by the door, his posture so rigid it seemed painful. The officer did not speak. He simply watched the old man with the blank, efficient gaze of a camera.

Asare did not look at the view. He watched the rain trace paths down the window, merging, separating, like the futures they had once imagined. The room was oppressively quiet, the thick carpet and drapes swallowing sound. He could feel the Tower around him, a living entity of humming wires and pumping fluids and anxious human hearts. It was a feeling he had known once before, in the belly of a ship crossing the Atlantic to that first, fateful Pan-African congress. A sense of being inside the engine of history, a tiny, fragile component in a vast, indifferent machine.

He closed his eyes, but the afterimage of Adeshina remained—the Colonel in his glorious uniform, sitting in that steel chair before the abyss, the leather notebook lying at his feet like a sacrificed animal. *You should read it.* The plea of a fool. Adeshina had not become a man who read poetry. He had become a man who wrote reality with decrees and bullets. The notebook would be archived, or burned. Its power was not in its words, but in the memory of the hands that wrote them. A memory now entombed in this steel and concrete monument to forgetting.

The door clicked open. Asare did not turn, expecting the silent officer or perhaps a servant with tasteless canapés.

“They told me you were a ghost. I see they undersold you.”

The voice was a woman’s, low, cultured, with a British boarding-school finish over a deeper, local granite. Asare turned.

She stood just inside the room, having dismissed the officer with a slight nod. She was perhaps forty, dressed not in uniform but in an elegant, severe dress of charcoal-grey silk, her hair braided into a complex, regal crown. Her beauty was sharp, intelligent, and currently aimed at him like a surgical instrument. He knew her, of course. Everyone in the nation knew her face from the carefully staged photographs in the state newspaper: Adesua Adeshina. The Colonel’s daughter. The Head of the Cultural Reclamation Directorate.

“Miss Adeshina,” Asare said, making to rise.

“Don’t.” She waved a hand, moving further into the room. She did not sit. She examined him as one would a curious, possibly dangerous artifact. “The architect in the flesh. Or what’s left of it. I read your early work at university. *Odes to the Unborn Nation*. Quite stirring. Quite naive.”

“Youth’s only crime is hope, madam,” Asare replied, settling back. “A crime I see your generation has chosen not to repeat.”

A flicker of something—annoyance, appreciation—crossed her face. She walked to the window, her silhouette cutting the city’s glow. “He saw you, then. My father.”

“He did.”

“And?”

“And we spoke of old times. He showed me his new house.”

Adesua turned from the window, her eyes sharp. “This is not a house. It is a statement. A weapon. A god. Pick your metaphor. It is the physical proof that his will is the state’s will. That is the only covenant that matters now.”

Asare studied her. She had her father’s eyes—the same penetrating, analytical darkness. But where his had become like obsidian, polished and impenetrable, hers were like smoked glass. You could see the fire behind them, distorted.

“You believe that,” he said, not a question.

“I *administer* that,” she corrected. “My directorate ensures every painting, every play, every school textbook aligns with the… aesthetic of the new dawn. We reclaim our culture from the colonizers, and then we reshape it to fit the foundations of this Tower. It is delicate work. The pruning of a national soul.” She tilted her head. “A ghost from the past, carrying a notebook of old promises, is not a helpful tool. It is a contaminant.”

“I did not come to be a tool. I came to be a witness.”

“To what? His triumph?” She gave a short, mirthless laugh. “You are a decade too late for tragedy, and a century too early for redemption. This is the interlude. The long, quiet reign.”

Asare felt a deep, weary sadness for her. “You have built your cage so well you no longer see the bars. You just polish them.”

Her composure tightened. “You speak of cages. You, who fled to the ultimate cage—neutrality. The gilded irrelevance of the Swiss academic. At least here, we are *doing* something. We are building. It is messy. It is bloody. It is real.”

“And what are you building, Adesua? A nation, or a museum dedicated to your father?”

The air left the room. For a moment, he saw not the powerful director, but a girl who had learned to calculate every word, every breath, for survival. The mask slipped, revealing the raw, ambitious, frightened architecture beneath.

“You are here as a courtesy,” she said, her voice dangerously soft. “A sentimental whim of a man who cannot afford sentiment. After tonight, you will disappear again. Back to your ghosts. This…” she gestured to encompass the Tower, the city, the nation, “…is the living world. And the living have to eat. They have to have lights that turn on. They have to have an enemy they can see, and a leader they can fear. Poetry does not secure borders. Memory does not generate electricity.”

“No,” Asare agreed quietly. “But it is the reason borders are worth securing. It is the light we try to generate. Without it, you are just a machine in the dark, humming to itself.”

She stared at him, a war raging behind her eyes. He saw her weighing him, deciding if he was a threat to be eliminated, a fool to be pitied, or a relic to be studied. He saw the moment her father’s training won. The smoke solidified into glass again.

“Enjoy the ceremony, Mr. Asare,” she said, moving towards the door. “The Midnight Sun is quite a spectacle. It is designed to be seen from miles away. To blot out the stars.” She paused, her hand on the knob. “Try not to look too closely at the source of the light. It can burn out your eyes.”

She left, closing the door with a soft, final click.

Asare was alone again with the silent officer and the weeping window. He leaned forward, his bones aching. He reached into the pocket of his *boubou* and pulled out a single, folded sheet of paper, brittle and yellowed. Not from the notebook. This was older. A leaf from a different tree.

It was a hand-drawn map, in faded ink. Not of streets or borders, but of the old city, the one that had existed before the boulevards, before the Tower. It showed the sacred grove of the *orisha* Osanyin, the healer. It showed the winding path to the hill where the first king was buried. It showed the course of the underground river that was said to be the city’s lifeblood, its secret heart.

He had not shown this to Adeshina. This was not a covenant of words. This was a covenant of place. Of earth.

The map showed the old king’s hill. The hill upon which the People’s Unity Tower now stood.

---

In the Grand Ballroom, six hundred people breathed the same air, a mixture of expensive perfume, anticipation, and a faint, metallic tang of fear. The orchestra had finished its hybrid symphony. Now, a chorus of children in pristine white robes sang a newly-composed anthem, their voices high and painfully pure. *“From the ashes of the past, we rise! Beneath the Midnight Sun, our future lies!”*

Colonel Adeshina stood on a low dais at the head of the room, a smile etched onto his face. It was not the fierce, charismatic grin of the revolutionary years. It was a calm, authoritative curve of the lips, practised in a mirror until it conveyed exactly the right mixture of strength and benevolence. He received the line of dignitaries—the handshakes firm and brief, the nods measured. The French ambassador murmured congratulations in fluent, oily French. The Soviet advisor crushed his hand and said something about “historical inevitability.” The World Bank delegation head, a pale man with a nervous sweat, talked of “structural adjustments” and “future partnerships.”

Adeshina heard none of it. He was listening to the Tower.

It had a voice, his Tower. A deep, sub-auditory hum, the aggregate of its ten thousand tons of steel and concrete, its climate systems, its elevators, its beating heart of generators in the sub-levels. He had come to know its moods. Tonight, it hummed with a strained, febrile energy. It was holding its breath.

His eyes scanned the crowd, not seeing faces, but positions, alignments, threats. General Okafor, head of the army, holding court by the champagne fountain, his laughter too loud. *Content for now, but his loyalty is a well with a shallow bottom.* The Minister of Resources, in deep conversation with the Chinese trade attaché. *Skimming his percentage, a manageable greed.* Major Ransom, standing apart near a pillar, watching everyone, her face a slate. *Loyal. For now, because she believes in the system, not the man.*

And Adesua, moving through the crowd like a sleek grey shark, exchanging pleasantries, her smile a perfect, calibrated tool. His daughter. His most brilliant and terrifying creation. She believed in the Tower absolutely. He wondered, with a cold spike of paternal clarity, if she would believe in him if he ever stood against it.

The children’s anthem reached its crescendo. The room burst into applause, a wave of sound that broke against the dais. Adeshina raised a hand, and silence fell, swift and total. This was the moment. The unveiling. The light.

“My friends,” he began, his voice amplified by hidden microphones, filling the vast space, rich and warm. “Compatriots. We stand tonight not at a pinnacle, but at a beginning. The beginning of a true, self-determined eternity…”

As he spoke the prepared words, his mind split. One part delivered the speech with flawless, resonant conviction. The other part was back in the sub-level chamber, looking at the notebook on the concrete floor. *The poem about the baobab. The night they killed Ekon.*

Ekon. The firebrand. The purist. The one who said compromise was a cancer. They had been hiding in a safe house in the old quarter, the air thick with mosquitoes and paranoia. Ekon had wanted to bomb the colonial governor’s residence. Adeshina had argued for patience, for building a broader coalition. They had fought, their whispers fierce in the dark. Kwame had tried to calm them, had written a poem about a baobab tree that could shelter all beneath its branches, that drew strength from deep, hidden waters.

The next day, Ekon was dead. Betrayed. Captured and shot in the public square. Adeshina had felt a terrible, clarifying grief, and beneath it, a colder, more practical relief. Ekon’s path would have led them all to the gallows. His death was a necessity. The first of many.

That night, he had taken Kwame’s notebook and added a line of his own beneath the baobab poem. He couldn’t remember the words now. Something about roots needing to strangle weaker plants to reach the sun.

“…and so, with pride and with unwavering resolve, I give to you—to our people—the light that will never set!” Adeshina’s voice rose, a conductor’s flourish.

He pressed a small, crystal button on the podium.

For a moment, nothing. A held breath.

Then, from the apex of the Tower high above them, a light erupted. It was not a gentle illumination. It was a violent, white-blue spear that lanced into the sky, cutting through the rain, scattering the clouds. It was blinding, even through the ballroom’s filtered windows. The Midnight Sun.

The crowd gasped, then applauded, a roar of approval and awe. The light pulsed, slowly rotating, a celestial scythe sweeping across the sleeping city. It was power made visible. It was dominance. It was beautiful, and it was terrible.

Adeshina smiled his etched smile, bathing in the adulation and the manufactured light.

In the viewing suite, Kwame Asare flinched as the light flooded the room, bleaching the colour from everything, casting stark, monstrous shadows. It was a light with no warmth. A light that revealed only surfaces, scorching away nuance. He looked down at the map in his hands, at the hill marked with the king’s burial symbol.

He knew, then, what the gift truly was. It was not the notebook. It was the map. And the gift was not for Adeshina.

It was for the land itself. A reminder of what lay buried beneath the foundation.

The silent officer by the door shifted, his hand going unconsciously to his holstered pistol as the strange, cold light washed over him. Asare folded the map away, back into the sanctuary of his pocket.

Outside, the rain continued to fall, but now the drops glittered like falling diamonds as they passed through the beam of the Midnight Sun—a cascade of false jewels, a beautiful, empty lie.

## **CHAPTER 3**

The false dawn of the Midnight Sun bled into a real, sullen morning. The rain had stopped, leaving the city steaming under a bruised sky. In the streets, the state-sponsored jubilation continued—tired dancers in traditional gear performed for Party officials, schoolchildren waved little paper flags, and from loudspeakers mounted on lampposts, the new anthem played on a loop. The air smelled of wet trash, diesel, and the sweet, cloying scent of *akara* being fried on street corners.

In the Leopard’s Den—the sprawling, fortified presidential suite on the Tower’s top floor—Colonel Adeshina was not celebrating. He was dissecting a report, his uniform tunic undone at the neck. The room was a study in controlled power: low-slung leather sofas, a desk carved from a single slab of zebrawood, and another of those monolithic windows. The Midnight Sun was off now, its purpose served. The view was of a city hungover from a spectacle it didn’t choose.

The report was from Major Ransom. It was concise, factual, and troubling. Kwame Asare’s border crossing had been facilitated. Not by incompetence, but by design. A Lieutenant in the eastern border guard, a man with unremarkable service records, had been paid a sum equivalent to two years’ salary. The money trail was a ghost—untraceable electronic transfers from a shell corporation based in Lagos. The lieutenant had vanished from his post this morning.

“A provocateur,” Adeshina said, not looking up from the page. “A whisper from our enemies. They dust off an old ghost, point him at me, and hope he stirs up the memories. A cheap trick.”

Major Ransom stood before the desk, her hands clasped behind her back. “Perhaps, sir. But cheap tricks are often the most effective. Asare is a symbol. His presence, his questions… they are a virus. They don’t attack the body of the state, they attack the idea of it. Ideas are harder to quarantine.”

Adeshina finally looked at her. “You sound like him.”

“I sound like a security officer assessing a non-ballistic threat. The French, the Soviets, the Americans—they have tanks and treaties. They are predictable. This… this is a spiritual insurgency. And your daughter’s directorate is ill-equipped to handle it.”

“My daughter,” Adeshina said slowly, “is handling the *celebration*.” He didn’t miss the slight emphasis Ransom had placed on ‘daughter.’ A reminder of a potential soft spot, a vulnerability. “What of Asare’s contacts since arrival? He did not come here just to see me.”

“None. He was brought directly from the border to a safe house in the old quarter. Our house. He was alone. He had only the clothes on his back and the notebook. We found nothing else on his person.”

*The map*, a voice whispered in Adeshina’s mind. *He had a map. He showed me with his eyes.* But he had said nothing of it to Ransom. Some secrets you kept even from your most trusted blade. Especially from them.

“And the notebook?” he asked.

“Archived. In Section Seven. As you ordered.”

He had not ordered that. He had left it on the floor. Someone had taken the initiative. Ransom? Or someone else who had access to that chamber? The thought was an ice pick to his temple.

“Bring it to me,” he said.

“Sir?”

“The notebook. Bring it. Now.”

Ransom’s flint eyes flickered, the only sign of her surprise. “Yes, Colonel.” She turned and left, her heels silent on the thick rug.

Alone, Adeshina rose and walked to the window. In the cold morning light, his masterpiece looked different. The geometric sectors seemed less like order and more like confinement. The wide boulevards were empty arteries. The Tower’s shadow, long and knife-like, cut across the old quarter, plunging the tangled streets into a premature twilight. He had always seen that shadow as a symbol of his reach, his protection. Now, for the first time, it looked like a stain.

His private line buzzed. The red phone. Only five people had the number. He picked it up.

“It’s done.” The voice on the other end was a gravelly whisper, familiar. General Okafor.

“The lieutenant?” Adeshina asked.

“Found in a ditch outside of Keti. A car accident. Very tragic. The money was not recovered.”

“Make sure the family is compensated. Generously. From the Widows’ Fund.”

“Of course.” A pause. Okafor was not a man for pauses. “Abayomi… this poet. He is a loose thread. In the old days, we knew how to trim loose threads.”

“The old days required butchers. Today requires surgeons.” Adeshina’s voice was flat. “He is a symbol. Symbols are not cut. They are… managed.”

“You manage a bull by keeping it out of the china shop. This bull is already in your parlour.” Another pause. “People are talking. Not the people in the ballroom. The people in the barracks. The junior officers. They hear a founder has returned. They hear he questioned you. They are wondering why he is not dead, or why he is not on the dais with you. Wonder is a cancer.”

Adeshina’s grip tightened on the phone. Okafor was telling him his own soldiers were infected with the virus. “Your job, General, is to cut cancers out. Do your job. And let me manage the symbols.”

He hung up before Okafor could reply. The threat was clear. Okafor’s loyalty was conditional on the perception of strength. Asare was undermining that perception simply by breathing.

The door opened. Ransom entered, holding the leather notebook. She placed it on the zebrawood desk. It looked small, insignificant, a dead thing.

“Leave it,” Adeshina said.

She left again. He stared at the notebook. He had not touched it since he took it from Asare’s hand. He reached out, his fingers hovering over the worn cover. He could almost feel the heat of that Paris café, the smell of cheap wine and cheaper cigarettes, the fever-dream energy of a future they were going to seize.

He opened it.

The pages were filled with Kwame’s elegant, looping script—in English, in French, in smatterings of their native tongue. Poems, diatribes, sketches of flags that would never fly. He flipped through, the pages whispering like dry leaves. He found the baobab poem. There it was, in Kwame’s hand, about shelter and deep roots.

And beneath it, in his own younger, angrier script, the line he had added:
*“But even the baobab must let the smaller trees die at its feet, that its shadow may be total, and its reign unchallenged.”*

He had written it as a justification for Ekon’s death. A law of nature. The strong must eclipse the weak for the forest to thrive. He had believed it then. He had built a philosophy of state upon it.

He kept flipping. Near the back, he found something that stopped his breath.

It was a sketch, not in Kwame’s hand, but in his own. A rough, architectural drawing. It was the first concept for this very Tower. But it was different. The one he had built was a needle, a spear. This sketch showed a structure that widened as it rose, like a tree, or a termite mound—organic, rooted. At its base, the sketch showed a public square, gardens, a hall for the people. At its pinnacle, a simple beacon, a flame, not a rotating searchlight.

He had drawn this. He had forgotten. He had shown it to Kwame, who had smiled and said, “Yes. A shelter. Not a weapon.”

He had dismissed the idea. A shelter could be breached. A weapon commanded fear. He had redrawn it, sharpened it, made it a statement.

A wave of nausea, profound and disorienting, washed over him. It was not guilt. He had long ago made peace with the necessary corpses. It was something worse: the vertigo of realizing a fundamental choice, made decades ago in a moment of pragmatic fury, had dictated every choice since. He had not been building a nation. He had been building a justification for that first, brutal choice. The Tower was not a symbol of independence. It was a monument to the man who chose the spear over the shelter.

The door burst open without a knock.

Adesua stood there, her composure shattered. Her usually impeccable braids were slightly frayed, and her eyes were wide with a raw, unvarnished fear he had not seen since she was a child.

“Father.”

“I am working.”

“The lights,” she said, her voice trembling. “In Sector Seven. The new hospital.”

“What about them?”

“They’re off. The whole sector. Since the Midnight Sun activated last night. The generators are online. The circuits are live. But the lights… won’t come on.”

A cold finger traced Adeshina’s spine. Sector Seven was a showpiece. A model community with a state-of-the-art hospital. Its power grid was independent, redundant. A blackout was impossible.

“A fault. A tripped relay. Send engineers.”

“I have. They say there is no fault. The power is there. It is flowing. But the lights… they say it’s as if the bulbs themselves are refusing it. As if the light is… being swallowed.”

*Swallowed.* The word hung in the air like a poison.

“And the people?” he asked, his voice dangerously quiet.

“They are in the streets. Not celebrating. Just… standing. Looking



---

# Chapter 4

## **CHAPTER 4**

The silence in the study was a physical presence, thick and cold, absorbing the frantic energy of Adesua’s words until only the dread remained, a sediment at the bottom of the room. Colonel Abayomi Adeshina did not move. He watched his daughter’s face, the elegant architecture of her composure cracked by this inexplicable fault line. The fear in her eyes was not for the dark. It was for the logic that had failed, the equation that no longer balanced. His world was built on such equations.

“Swallowed,” he repeated, the word a dry, foreign object in his mouth.

He turned from her, his gaze sweeping over the vast, detailed map of the capital mounted on the wall behind his desk. Sector Seven glowed a soft, optimistic green, a jewel in his crown of concrete and steel. The hospital was a tiny, perfect square. His finger, blunt and heavy with its gold signet ring, came to rest on it. The leather of his glove whispered against the glass.

“Mobilize the Third Engineering Battalion,” he said, his voice returning to its bedrock register, a sound of compressed earth and old stone. “Full diagnostic sweep. Not a fault. A *sabotage*. Trace it. I want the pattern before the next hour.”

“Father, the engineers insist it is not—”

“The engineers diagnose machines,” he cut her off, finally looking at her. The Leopard was in his eyes now, the cold, assessing gleam that had frozen the blood of generals. “I diagnose men. This is a pattern. A statement. Someone is demonstrating that they can touch my jewel. That they can make my light… disobey.”

Adesua held his stare, the child’s fear receding, pressed down by the weight of her training. She gave a sharp, military nod. “Yes, sir.”

As she turned to leave, his voice stopped her at the threshold. “Adesua.”

She looked back.

“Bring me the original schematics for the Sector Seven grid. And the personnel files for every technician who has laid a hand on it in the last six months. Ours *and* the foreign consultants.”

When the door clicked shut, the silence returned, but it was different now. It was a silence he had filled with purpose. The vertigo of moments before—the sickening glimpse of the shelter he had not built—was a luxury he could not afford. The past was a country he had razed. The present was a grid, a system, a flow of power. And something was interrupting the flow.

He moved to the great teak cabinet beside the map, unlocked it with a key from a chain around his neck. Inside were no guns, no gold. There were files, brittle with age, and a single, long object wrapped in oiled canvas. He ignored the files. His hands, steady now, unwrapped the canvas.

It was an *ada*, a ceremonial sword of his grandfather’s time. The blade was not the showpiece steel of parades; it was old iron, dark and water-patterned from the forge, its edge still whispering of a forgotten sharpness. The hilt was bound in leather worn smooth by a dead man’s sweat. It was a weapon, yes, but of a different language. It spoke of directness, of a hand’s breadth of violence, of accountability. It did not speak from a tower.

He hefted its weight. It felt more real than the pistol at his hip.

This was the preparation. Not the barking of orders—that was routine. This was the quiet, internal severing. The choice to step out from behind the desk, the radio, the layers of insulated command. The Tower justified everything, but it also distanced everything. The darkness in Sector Seven was not a report to be read; it was a place. A place where his light had failed. To understand a rot, you had to touch the wood.

He placed the *ada* on the desk. From a drawer, he took a simple, rugged satellite phone, a device untouched by the capital’s networked grid. He dialed a number from memory, a number that bypassed all official channels.

It was answered on the first ring. No greeting.
“I am coming out,” Adeshina said.
A pause on the line, then a single, guttural word. “Where?”
“The jewel has a crack. I will see it for myself.”
“The car?”
“No. The motorcycle.”

He ended the call. The black Mercedes was a fortress, a statement. It announced the Leopard from a kilometer away. The old BMW R75 motorcycle in the secure garage beneath the compound was a ghost. It was from his guerrilla days, maintained in perfect, mute readiness by one loyal, silent mechanic. It was a machine for entering places, not for being received by them.

This was the departure. He shrugged off his tailored tunic, with its braid and medals, its weight of manufactured legacy. From a locked chest, he took a worn, olive-drab field jacket, its canvas faded, a faint, ghostly outline where different insignia had once been sewn. He pulled it on. It smelled of dust, petrol, and a life that had not yet calcified into myth.

He took the *ada* and slid it into a long, modified sheath sewn inside the jacket’s spine, the hilt resting at the nape of his neck. He checked the pistol, a Browning Hi-Power, utilitarian and reliable. He left the gold signet ring on the desk, on top of the forgotten sketch of a shelter. The man who wore that ring commanded the Tower. The man in the field jacket would go to the dark.

He left the study without a backward glance, passing through the hushed, opulent corridors of the presidential wing. He was a shadow against the marble, a dissonant note in the symphony of contained power. The servants he passed did not look at him; they felt him, and their eyes went carefully, fearfully blank. He was crossing a boundary they instinctively understood.

The garage was a cool, concrete cavern smelling of grease and solitude. The motorcycle stood where it always stood, under a dust cover. He pulled the cover off. The machine was a testament to brutal simplicity: black, boxer engine, a tool for movement, nothing more. He mounted it, the old leather of the seat groaning in familiar protest. He kick-started it, and the engine roared to life, a raw, animal sound that was swallowed by the concrete. It was a sound from before the speeches, before the Midnight Sun.

He rode out into the night. The rain had ceased, leaving the city glistening under the artificial dawn of the Tower’s beacon. The light was a physical pressure, a bleach that erased shadows, turning the world into a flat, colorless replica of itself. He turned away from its heart, gunning the engine down back avenues and service roads, a specter slipping through the capital’s arteries.

The transition was not gradual. It was a wall.

One moment he was on a clean, lit street, the Tower’s glow a blanket. The next, he turned a corner into Sector Seven, and the world vanished.

The darkness was absolute. It was not the soft dark of a power cut, where shapes linger and the moon offers charity. This was a profound, hungry void. The Tower’s light, so omnipotent everywhere else, stopped at the sector boundary as if sliced by a knife. It did not fade; it ended. Here, the air itself felt thick, resistant. His motorcycle headlight, a beam that usually cut a swath through the night, pushed out only a few feeble meters before its light seemed to dissolve, absorbed by the nothingness.

He slowed the bike to a crawl. This was the new world. Its first rule was the negation of his primary tool: illumination. Its second rule announced itself immediately.

The streets were not empty. They were full of people.

They stood on sidewalks, in doorways, in the middle of the road. They did not speak. They did not move. They simply stood, facing inward, toward the heart of the sector where the hospital should have been. Their faces, caught in the pathetic remnant of his headlight, were not panicked. They were blank, slack, as if waiting for a signal. They did not blink at his approach. They parted just enough for his motorcycle to pass, a silent, organic tide, their movements eerily synchronized. The sound of his engine was muffled, deadened, as if the darkness were also a soundproofing shroud.

A deep, atavistic alarm stirred in his gut. This was not a protest. This was a trance. He was not a leader here; he was an intrusion.

He navigated by memory and the faint, ghostly green glow of a battery-powered compass affixed to his handlebars. The hospital was eight blocks in. The further he went, the thicker the silence became, broken only by the wet crunch of his tires on debris and the low, steady thrum of the bike—a heartbeat in a corpse.

He reached the hospital plaza. His headlight swept across the scene and died.

The hospital was a monolithic slab of modern design, now a cliff-face of black glass. Before it, in the vast concrete square, thousands of people stood in perfect, concentric rings, utterly still. And in the center, where the ceremonial flagpole stood, a figure waited.

It was not a soldier. It was a man in the simple, practical coveralls of a city electrical worker. He stood under a standard-issue emergency lantern, which should have cast a pool of yellow light. Instead, the lantern emitted a faint, sickly grey nimbus that illuminated nothing but the man’s own calm, smiling face. In his hands was not a weapon, but a bulky, old-fashioned multimeter, its probes dangling.

This was the threshold guardian.

Adeshina killed the engine. The silence that rushed in was a weight. He dismounted, the crunch of his boots on gravel absurdly loud. He walked toward the center, the sea of people parting for him without a sound, their eyes tracking him with a vacant, collective focus.

The man in the coveralls watched him come, the smile not touching his eyes. “Colonel,” he said. His voice was ordinary, conversational, yet it carried perfectly in the muffled air. “You are off-grid. We were not expecting you in person.”

Adeshina stopped ten paces away. He could feel the *ada* against his spine. “You know me.”

“We know the signature,” the man said, tapping his multimeter. “The hunger. The draw. It’s unique. Like a fingerprint in the current.” He looked around at the standing crowd, with a technician’s pride. “You built a network to feed your Sun. A beautiful, greedy thing. We simply… found a sink. A place for all that hungry light to go.”

“Who is ‘we’?” Adeshina’s hand rested near his hip.

“The ones who remember the old lines,” the man said. “The paths of power you paved over. You thought you were building a new world. You were just laying a very expensive table for us.” He tilted his head. “Your daughter is very efficient. Her engineers will find nothing. Because nothing is broken. The power is flowing. It is just flowing *here*.” He gestured to the ground beneath him. “Into the deep. Into the old shelter you didn’t build.”

The word was a spike in Adeshina’s ear. *Shelter*.

“What do you want?” Adeshina asked, his voice low, a rumble in the static-filled dark.

“The Tower is a weapon,” the man said, his smile finally fading. “It draws too much. It screams into the dark. We cannot allow it. Turn it off. Dismantle the beacon.”

“Or?”

The man shrugged, a gesture of terrible, casual power. “Or we will continue to siphon your light. Sector by sector. Block by block. We will show your people that your dawn is a lie, that it can be taken away by men you never see. We will return you to the dark you came from.” He looked past Adeshina, at the mute, obedient thousands. “They are not afraid of the dark, Colonel. They are waiting to see who can hold it. And who can take the light away.”

This was the test. Not of force, but of premise. His entire rule was built on the provision of light, of order, of the visible. This man offered a different corruption: not the seizure of power, but its negation. A promise of anonymity, of a power that worked not by shining, but by swallowing.

Adeshina took a step forward. The crowd tightened almost imperceptibly. “You are a thief in a blackout,” he said. “You have no vision. Only an appetite.”

“Vision requires light,” the guardian said softly. “And here, we decide where the light goes.”

Adeshina did not draw his gun. Guns were for visible enemies. He moved with the shocking, liquid speed that had earned his name. Two long strides closed the distance. His hand did not go for the Browning; it swept over his shoulder and down, the old iron *ada* flashing from its sheath not with a shine, but with a dull, grey streak.

The man’s eyes widened, not in fear, but in surprise at the anachronism, the brutal simplicity of the gesture. He raised the multimeter like a talisman.

The *ada* did not strike him. It swept down in a clean, vicious arc and severed the cable connecting the emergency lantern to its battery pack.

The sickly grey nimbus died instantly.

In the perfect, absolute blackness that followed, Adeshina’s other hand shot out, found the man’s coveralls, and yanked him forward. He spoke, his mouth an inch from the man’s ear, his voice a subsonic growl that belonged to the oldest dark.

“I did not come from the dark,” Adeshina whispered. “I *am* the dark. And I do not need your light to see you.”

He heard the man’s breath catch, the cool certainty shattering. The multimeter clattered on the concrete.

Then, from the depths of the crowd, a new sound began. A low, resonant hum, not from a machine, but from a hundred, then a thousand throats. It was a sound of recognition, of a deeper, older frequency being struck. The standing people did not move, but the quality of their stillness changed. It was no longer a waiting. It was a listening.

The guardian in his hands went rigid. “You… you can’t. This is not the way…”

“You wanted the old lines,” Adeshina said, shoving him back. “You found one.”

He turned, sheathing the *ada*. He did not look back at the man scrambling in the blackness, his authority broken not by a greater light, but by a more complete dark. Adeshina walked to his motorcycle, the hum of the crowd a vibrating floor beneath his feet. He kick-started the engine. This time, the roar was not swallowed. It echoed in the plaza, a challenge.

His headlight flared to life. And this time, the darkness did not swallow it. It pushed back, just a few more meters, carving a tunnel of weak, but real, light through the pressing void. The people in its path blinked, stirred, as if waking from a dream.

He turned the bike, pointing it back the way he came. He had seen the crack. He had met its guardian. He had passed the test not with the Tower’s power, but by reaching for something older and more personal that he had long ago buried. The victory was acid in his mouth. To save his light, he had to remember how to wield the dark.

As he rode slowly back through the parting crowd, the hook of dread settled deeper, past strategy, into soul. This was not a battle for a city. It was a battle for the premise of his life. The enemy was not in the shadows. The enemy was the shadow he had cast, given form and appetite and a technician’s smile. And it was feeding on his sun.

He crossed the boundary out of Sector Seven, and the Tower’s false dawn washed over him again, cold and accusing. He did not feel its warmth. He felt only the chill of the true dark at his back, and the heavier, more familiar cold of the iron against his spine. The Midnight Sun was a target. And he had just shown the things that lived in the deep exactly where he kept his shelter.



---

# Chapter 5

## **CHAPTER 5**

The silence in the Tower’s command suite was a different texture of dark. It was sterile, pressurized, humming with the suppressed energy of a hundred dormant machines. The wall of monitors showed the city from a god’s remove: the geometric glow of the Midnight Sun’s sectors, the pulsing arteries of the boulevards, the vast, patient blot of Sector Seven. Colonel Adeshina stood before the central display, his back to the room. On the glass, his own reflection was a ghost imposed over the map, a man-shaped cutout of nothing.

He had not reported the incident. He had logged a routine perimeter reconnaissance, cited “localized atmospheric interference” for the comms blackout. The lie was a pebble in his boot, a small, precise agony with every step. The truth was a live wire he had coiled and shoved into his own chest. He could feel it sparking against his ribs, threatening to fuse with the older, colder metal there.

Captain Ikenna entered, his boots a soft crush on the polymer floor. He held a data-slate like a shield. “Colonel. The Phase Two consumption metrics from the Northern Grid.”

Adeshina did not turn. “Read them.”

“They are… optimal. Ninety-seven percent efficiency. Citizen energy quotas are being met with a twelve percent surplus. The feedback from the residential blocks is…” Ikenna searched for the approved word. “Gratifying.”

“Gratifying.” Adeshina tasted the word. It was flavorless, a nutrient paste of language. On the map, the Northern Grid glowed a serene, consistent blue. A perfect, docile organ. He pointed a finger, not at the blue, but at the edge of the black blot. “And the buffer zone here? Between Sector Seven and Grid North?”

Ikenna shifted. The data-slate chirped. “Passive drain is within expected parameters. The dampening field is stable. The… the folk there are compliant. They draw their rationed lumens. No disturbances.”

“No disturbances,” Adeshina echoed. He finally turned. Ikenna’s face was a careful study in loyalty, but his eyes, the whites too visible, were the eyes of a man listening for a tremor in the earth. “You sound uncertain, Captain.”

“It is only the numbers, sir. The drain is consistent, but the *pattern*… it is a flat line. No peaks, no troughs. Even in a coma, a brain shows some variation. This is the readout of a dead battery. Or a full one.” Ikenna offered the slate. “It is likely a sensor error. I will dispatch a technician.”

Adeshina took the slate. The graph was a horizon, perfectly straight. A thing that did not exist in nature. He thought of the crowd in the plaza, their stillness that became a listening. A flat line was a kind of listening too. A held breath. “No technician. I will go.”

“Sir, a Colonel does not—”

“A Colonel does what the light requires,” Adeshina cut him off, the official phrase ash in his mouth. “I will assess the sensor grid personally. Prepare a jeep. Non-marked. And Ikenna?”

“Sir.”

“Bring the Type-76 emitter. The portable one.”

Ikenna’s breath hitched, a tiny fracture in his composure. The Type-76 was not a tool for assessment. It was a surgical instrument, a lance of focused dawn-light capable of burning a man’s shadow into the wall behind him. “Regulation requires a Level-Three threat designation for armed emitter deployment, Colonel.”

Adeshina looked at his ghost on the map, overlaid on the black blot. “Consider it brought for calibration. In this new world, Captain, we must constantly recalibrate.”

***

The jeep was a boxy, sand-colored thing, the Tower’s insignia absent from its doors. It felt flimsy after the Pullman’s tank-like weight. Adeshina drove, Ikenna rigid beside him, the Type-76 in its polished ceramite case across their laps like a coffin for a star. They left the Tower’s radiant precincts, the false sun warming the hood, then passed into the long, architectural shadows of the mid-city canyons. Finally, they crossed into the buffer.

It was a place of perpetual twilight. The Tower’s light here was filtered, diluted, a grudging concession piped through frosted conduits that ran along the streets like exposed vertebrae. The buildings were lower, older, their facades stained by the memory of a real sun. People moved here, but their movements were economical, hushed. They did not look at the jeep. They looked at the ground six feet ahead of their own feet, as if following a faint, private luminescence.

Adeshina stopped at a sensor node, a sleek pillar of brushed steel crowned with a crystalline receptor. It was an island of Tower-tech in the granular decay of the street. The diagnostic panel glowed green. All optimal.

“See, sir?” Ikenna said, his voice low. “A ghost in the machine. A frozen loop in the data-stream.”

Adeshina did not touch the node. He looked at the building opposite. A three-story tenement, its windows patched with cardboard and plastic sheeting. On the second floor, a shutter was broken, hanging by one hinge. The darkness within was absolute, a square of velvet nothing. But as he watched, he saw it. A faint, rhythmic pulse. Not a light. A *deepening*. A slow, tidal breath of pure dark.

“Wait here,” he said.

“Colonel, the protocol—”

“Is a prayer for a god that isn’t listening,” Adeshina said, and his voice was so quiet it silenced Ikenna more effectively than a shout. He opened the case. The Type-76 lay in its housing, a smooth, cold cylinder of alloy with a lens of black glass. He lifted it out. It was heavier than it looked. It had the dense, patient gravity of a collapsed sun.

He crossed the street. The tenement door was not locked; it sagged inward, its wood spongy with damp. The interior smelled of mildew, boiled cassava, and underneath it, the sharp, clean scent of ozone—the smell of spent energy, of a vacuum waiting to be filled. He climbed the stairs. They groaned under his weight, a sound that seemed to travel not up, but down, into the foundation. The pulsing dark was from a door at the end of the second-floor hall.

He did not knock. He placed his palm against the flaking paint and pushed.

The room was bare. A single, gutted candle on the floor. In the center, cross-legged, sat a woman. She was perhaps sixty, her hair a cloud of white, her face a topography of deep lines. She wore a simple *buba* of faded indigo. Before her, on the floor, was not a machine, but a child’s toy: a small, tin windmill, its paint chipped. It was not moving. The air was still.

But the darkness in the room was alive. It pooled around her, thick as ink, and with every slow breath she took, it swelled and receded, swelled and receded, in perfect, silent rhythm with the sensor node’s flat line outside. She was not drawing power from the grid. She was *matching* it. Nullifying it. Creating a perfect equilibrium of zero.

She opened her eyes. They were not the milky eyes of a blind elder. They were clear, deep brown, and they held the reflection of the lens of the Type-76 without fear.

“You are the Leopard,” she said. Her voice was dry, papery, the sound of leaves turning over. “You carry a little sun in your hand. But you walk in your own shadow. It is a long shadow. It has weight.”

Adeshina felt the *ada* at his hip, a cold tooth against his thigh. He felt the older, deeper cold in his spine. He did not raise the emitter. “You are disrupting the grid.”

“I am listening to it,” she corrected. “It sings a single, hungry note. I sing back the silence it left behind. A balance. For now.” Her gaze drifted to the toy windmill. “My grandson. He is in the Northern Grid. In a school of light. They teach him to crave the glow, to fear the dim. He forgets the wind. He forgets how to make his own breeze.” She touched the tin blade, and for a second, the pervasive dark in the room flexed, and the blade turned, once, with a faint, squealing rasp. Then it was still. “I hold the balance so he has something to come back to. A memory of a different kind of power.”

This was not a guardian of a crack. This was a weaver. A preserver. A minor, desperate magician of null space. She was the first rule of survival in this new world: power did not just corrupt the wielder; it corrupted the very medium it moved through, until resistance became a subtle, patient art of stillness. She was not an enemy. She was a lesson.

“They will find you,” Adeshina said. “Their sensors will eventually differentiate your silence from a malfunction. They will send more than a man with a calibration tool.”

“They sent you,” she said, smiling faintly. “Is that not more?”

He had no answer. He looked at the Type-76 in his hand. Its purpose was to scour, to purify, to burn away anomalous shadow. To turn this room, this woman, into a brief, bright scream of light and then a cinder. It would be clean. Efficient. Gratifying.

He lowered the emitter. “You can’t hold it forever.”

“Nothing is forever,” she said. “Not even the sun at midnight. I am buying a boy time. It is what the old do. We trade our breath for the future’s chance.” She closed her eyes again, returning to her meditation, to the slow, tidal breath of dark. The interview was over.

Adeshina backed out of the room. He closed the door. The hallway seemed brighter, vulgar. He returned to the jeep, placed the Type-76 back in its case. The click of the latch was the loudest sound in the world.

“Well, sir?” Ikenna asked, eager for a technical solution, for an order to fix.

“The node is faulty,” Adeshina said, starting the engine. “Log it for replacement. Low priority.”

He drove. The woman’s words coiled inside him. *You walk in your own shadow. It has weight.* He had gone to confront an anomaly and found a mirror. Her resistance was passive, a spiritual sink. His had been active, a general’s calculated dissent. But both were reactions to the same devouring light. An alliance was forming, not of shared purpose, but of shared recognition. She was his first true companion in this, a guide to the grammar of quiet ruin.

***

The summons came two hours later, not through channels, but via a plain envelope delivered by a mute, grey-clad courier to the jeep as they parked beneath the Tower. The paper was thick, cream. The ink was dark blue. It bore a single symbol: a stylized, radiant eye. The Office of Harmonic Integration. Dr. Kemi Fadare.

Ikenna stared at it as if it were a venomous insect. “She does not summon Colonels. She suggests. She requests.”

“Today, she summons,” Adeshina said. He felt no surprise. The Tower saw everything. Perhaps it had seen his hesitation in the tenement. Perhaps it had felt the weight of the unused emitter. “Return the equipment to the armory. Say nothing.”

Dr. Fadare’s office was not in the military spire, but in the heart of the civic complex, a dome of milky glass that glowed from within like a cultured pearl. The air here was scentless, temperature-perfect. The sound of Adeshina’s boots was swallowed by a deep, sound-absorbent carpet the color of brain matter. Her door was open.

She stood before a floor-to-ceiling window, her back to him, silhouetted against the diffuse glow of the dome. She was a tall, slender woman in a suit of pale grey linen, her hair braided close to her skull. She did not turn.

“Colonel Adeshina. Thank you for coming.” Her voice was mellifluous, educated, devoid of accent. It was the voice of the state broadcaster, perfected. “I have been reviewing the metabolic readouts of our great project. They are, as you know, exceptional. The body politic is thriving. Its pulse is strong and regular.”

He said nothing. He stood just inside the door, a soldier in a temple of theory.

“Yet, a body can thrive while hosting a parasite,” she continued, turning now. Her face was striking, ageless, with high cheekbones and eyes the warm brown of polished teak. They held no warmth. They were analytical instruments. “A silent, symbiotic thing that feeds on the host’s energy, mimicking its rhythms, giving nothing back. It can live for years, undetected, until the host, inexplicably, weakens.”

She gestured to a chair. He remained standing. She smiled, a thin movement of lips.

“Your foray into Sector Seven was… unorthodox. But informative. You encountered a primitive immune response. A local antibody, clinging to a memory of a different biological state. You neutralized it. Effectively.” She picked up a data-pad, swiped. A hazy, thermal image appeared on the window-glass: his confrontation with the guardian, a blur of red and orange in a sea of cool blue. “You used a frequency outside our spectrum. A fascinating adaptation. A reversion to an ancestral toolkit.”

She had watched. Of course she had watched. The entire city was her petri dish.

“It was efficient,” he said, his voice a gravel scrape in the polished room.

“Efficiency is one metric,” she said, putting the pad down. “Harmony is another. Your action was efficient. It was not harmonious. It introduced a discordant note. A memory of violence. Of *personal* power.” She said the word ‘personal’ as if it were a pathogen. “The guardian is broken. But the crack he tended… it remains. And it has learned. It has tasted your unique signature. The shadow you wield.”

She walked toward him, stopping just outside the range of his instinctual strike zone. She smelled of verbena and sterile glass. “We are not enemies, Colonel. We are gardeners. The Midnight Sun is our cultivation lamp. We are growing a new human consciousness, one free from the chaotic, wasteful day-night cycles of fear and want. But a garden has weeds. Some are obvious. Some are deep-rooted, mimicking the crop.” She tilted her head. “The woman in the buffer tenement. She is a weed of the second kind. A null-weed. She does not fight the light. She pretends to be its absence. She is more dangerous than the screamer in the plaza.”

Adeshina’s blood turned to ice slurry in his veins. His face showed nothing. The Tower’s eye saw everything.

“You left her,” Fadare said, not accusing, observing. “You had the tool to cauterize that null-point. You chose not to. Was it sentiment? The old preying on the loyalty of the old?”

“It was assessment,” he forced out. “A single null-point is data. Eliminating it destroys the data. I deemed her a low-priority observation target. A potential lead to other anomalies.”

Fadare’s smile returned, wider this time. It did not touch her eyes. “Excellent. The military mind. Pragmatic. Long-view. This is why you are valuable. You think in terms of networks, not individual nodes.” She turned back to the window. “You are correct. She is a node. The null-weed has a root system. I want you to find it. Map it. And then, Colonel, you will purge it. Not with your shadow. With our light. You will prove that the Leopard can be retrained. That his unique capacities can be… harmonized.”

This was the clear antagonist. Not a warlord, not a rebel chieftain. A technician of souls. Her opposition was not to his goals, but to his essence. She did not fear his rebellion; she sought to co-opt his corruption, to turn his inner darkness into a scalpel for her brighter, cleaner world. The battle was no longer for the city, or even for his soul’s premise. It was for the right to be a flaw. The right to be a shadow.

“And if I refuse?” he asked, the question hanging in the dead air.

She did not turn. “Then you become a weed yourself, Colonel. And I will send a different gardener. One without your sentimental hesitation. One who will start the weeding with a boy in a school in the Northern Grid, and the old woman who breathes darkness for him.”

The hook of dread was no longer a hook. It was a cage, tightening around the core of him. She had given him an ally, then immediately made that ally his weapon. She had identified his weakness—not his power, but the atrophied muscle of his mercy—and was now exercising it, strengthening it for her own use.

“I will need resources,” he said, his voice hollow, the sound of a man agreeing to his own dissection.

“Of course,” she said, the victory absolute in her toneless voice. “You will have them. You are our best hunter, Abayomi. Our most lethal instrument. Go and hunt. Bring me the root of the silence.”

***

The test came that night. It came not from Fadare, but from the silence itself.

Adeshina stood on the balcony of his quarters, high in the military spire. The false dawn of the Midnight Sun turned the sky a perpetual bruised purple. Below, the city hummed, a docile, luminous beast. He held a glass of amber whisky, untouched. The ice had melted. He was thinking of roots.

A vibration began in the stone beneath his feet. Not a tremor. A sub-audible thrum, a bass note from the world’s foundation. The glass in his hand shivered, chiming softly. Across the city, for one heartbeat, two, the luminous grid flickered.

Not a dimming. A *stutter*.

It was gone so fast he might have imagined it. But the hum of the city did not return to its previous pitch. It was sharper, laced with a new anxiety. The Tower’s light burned, steady, accusing.

His balcony comm-unit buzzed. Ikenna’s voice, stripped of all military cadence, raw with panic. “Colonel. Sector Seven. The boundary. It’s… moving.”

Adeshina was already moving, the whisky glass left behind, a forgotten sacrament. “Define ‘moving.’”

“The darkness, sir. It’s *advancing*. The dampening field at Grid North buffer is failing. The black is just… swallowing the light. It’s not an outage. It’s an *erasure*. And sir… the spectral readouts from the encroachment zone. They’re showing a frequency resonance.” Ikenna’s voice broke. “It’s yours. The signature from the plaza.”

The root of the silence was not a network of old women. It was the crack. And it had learned. It had tasted his shadow, his reversion to the old, personal power, and it had found a way to replicate it. He had not just shown the deep where he kept his shelter. He had given it the key. His corruption was no longer just a spiritual burden; it had been weaponized, turned against the very system he was sworn to protect, by a force that understood the corrupting nature of power better than he did.

He had forged an alliance with a memory. He had identified an antagonist in a technician. Now, the major test was here. The thing he had unleashed was at the gate, wearing his face, singing his frequency. To fight it, he would have to use the very light that sought to hollow him out. To survive, he would have to become the harmonious instrument Fadare desired. The victory would be her victory. The alternative was annihilation.

He grabbed his sidearm, the *ada*, and ran for the door. The chill in his spine was now a glacial river, and the false dawn on his face felt like the first touch of a coming, absolute night. He had to wield the sun to fight his own shadow. And in doing so, he would complete his own corruption, becoming the perfect, lethal gardener of a world where no wild, dark thing—and no flawed, human soul—was allowed to grow.



---

# Chapter 6

## **CHAPTER 6**

The city’s arteries were clogged with a new kind of silence. It was not the absence of sound, but the consumption of it. The rain had stopped, leaving behind a sky the color of a fresh bruise and streets that gleamed with a sick, oily sheen. The Pullman moved through the near-empty boulevards like a shark through a black reef, its headlights carving tunnels in a fog that seemed to drink the light. Colonel Abayomi “The Leopard” Adeshina watched the city pass, not as a protector, but as a pathologist. The symptoms were everywhere. Streetlights flickered not with power loss, but with a slow, rhythmic dimming, as if something were breathing them in and out. The few citizens out at this hour moved with a stiff, marionette haste, their eyes fixed on the ground, refusing to look toward the northern districts where the sky was a solid, starless matte.

Captain Ikenna was a silhouette of tension in the front seat. He had the secure field comm pressed to his ear, his reports delivered in a staccato whisper. “Buffer perimeter at Grid North-Seven is gone. Not breached. *Absent*. The dampeners didn’t fail, sir. The readings indicate they were… metabolized. The black is advancing at approximately two meters per minute. It is generating a field that matches your spectral signature at ninety-seven percent fidelity.”

Adeshina did not respond. He felt the truth of it in the marrow of his bones, a cold, parasitic resonance. The crack in the world had tasted the particular vintage of his power—the old, personal violence, the shadow he had wielded in the plaza—and had begun to ferment its own. His corruption was no longer a private rot. It was a broadcast, a template for the void. He had handed the deep not just a key, but a blueprint.

“The Tower,” Adeshina said, his voice a low rasp in the chilled air.

“Fadare is locked down. Protocol Midnight Sun is active. She is… insulated.” Ikenna’s pause was eloquent. It said *for now*. It said *from you, as well*.

“Not the Tower. The source. The stronghold.” Adeshina’s mind, honed by decades of hunting insurgents in jungle and desert, parsed the tactical horror. The boundary wasn’t just moving. It was *rooting*. An incursion sought territory. A root sought dominion. “It will have a core. A nexus from which it propagates the resonance. Find it.”

“The spectral mapping is impossible. The interference is total. It’s like tracing a single drop of ink in an ocean of the same ink.”

“Then we do not trace the ink.” Adeshina leaned forward, the leather sighing beneath him. “We trace the vessel that first held it. The technician. Kemi. She spoke of a ‘source tap’ in the old hydro-grid. A place where the silence first pooled. Where she heard the song.” He saw it then, the map not of light, but of absence. The city’s old, forgotten veins. “It will be underground. A place of old power and older water. A place where a crack would not be seen, only felt.”

Ikenna was already working the tactical slate, his fingers flying. “Cross-referencing decommissioned infrastructure with the initial anomalous signal origins from the Ministry logs… There is a primary candidate. The Isale Pumping Station. Built during the First Republic. A cathedral for water, they called it. Decommissioned fifteen years ago when the new system came online. It lies… here.” A point glowed on the map, a red eye directly in the path of the advancing black, just inside the former boundary. “It is the root of the old grid. A perfect anchor.”

“And a perfect tomb,” Adeshina said. He felt the glacial river in his spine widen. The decision was upon him, stark and terrible. To fight a shadow wearing his face, he would need a light not his own. He would have to become the instrument. “We go there. Assemble the team. Not the regulars. The quiet ones. The ones who have seen cracks before and know how to seal them. And get me a link to Fadare’s harmonic archive. I need a frequency. Something pure. Something that cuts.”

Ikenna turned, his face pale in the dashboard glow. “Sir. To wield a harmonic against a resonance that mirrors your own… the feedback, the psychic recoil…”

“Will be my concern, Captain.” Adeshina’s smile was a thin, cold line. “Our concern is the city. The victory will be her victory. The method will be my damnation. Now move.”

---

**Scene 1**

The safehouse was not a place, but a memory. A pharmacist’s shop in the Old Quarter, its shelves still lined with dusty jars of herbs, its air thick with the ghosts of camphor and mint. In the back room, under a single, bare bulb, the team coalesced. They were not soldiers. They were archivists of violence.

There was Bello, whose hands were scarred from handling unstable spiritual capacitors during the Sect Wars. He sat perfectly still, his breathing measured, calibrating his inner silence. There was Chioma, a former geologist for the National Resource Corps who had mapped seismic tremors of both the earth and the soul; she studied the hydro-architectural schematics of Isale Station as if reading a liturgy of pressure and collapse. And there was the old man, Eze. He had no other name. He was a *dibia* of the new world, a mechanic of the unseen who had once, it was whispered, sealed a minor rift in the Benin fiber-optic trunk line with a chant and a soldering iron. He watched Adeshina with milky, knowing eyes.

“The song it sings is yours, Leopard,” Eze said, his voice like dry leaves scraping stone. “But it is a copy. A recording on rotten wire. It has the shape, not the substance. It knows your violence, but not the reason for it. Not the man who holds it.”

“The reason is irrelevant,” Adeshina said, checking the charge on the harmonic emitter Ikenna had procured. It was a sleek, silver cylinder, devoid of markings—one of Fadare’s pure instruments. It felt alien in his hand, a scalpel offered to a butcher. “It has the effect. It is unraveling our defenses by mimicking their commander’s spiritual signature. A perfect psychological weapon.”

“It is more,” Chioma said, not looking up from her schematics. “It is a geological event. The resonance is altering the physical properties of the environment. Turning reinforced concrete brittle, causing alloys to resonate into fatigue failure. It is not just pretending to be you, Colonel. It is using the *idea* of you as a catalytic agent for entropy.”

Bello spoke, his eyes still closed. “Then we cannot oppose force with force. A louder song will only hasten the collapse. We must introduce a counter-harmonic. A dissonance so precise it unravels the copy at its source.”

“A targeted corruption of the corruption,” Adeshina said. The irony was a bitter taste on his tongue. “Using Fadare’s light.”

“Using your knowledge of the original,” Eze corrected. “You must feed the emitter a frequency that is the *antithesis* of your shadow. Not your light—you have little. The antithesis of your violence is not peace. It is purpose. The reason you hold the *ada*. The thing you protect beneath the violence. That is the note it cannot mimic.”

Adeshina was silent. The thing beneath the violence was a locked room he had not entered in twenty years. A garden choked with weeds. A memory of a laugh, cut short. He had buried it under duty, under the weight of the nation, under the persona of the Leopard. To dig it up now was a vulnerability more terrifying than the advancing dark.

“We will infiltrate via the original maintenance conduits,” he said, ignoring Eze, pointing to Chioma’s map. “Here. The overflow channel. It will be flooded, but it bypasses the main structural wards. We move quickly, in silence. Our objective is the central pump chamber. If this is the root, the nexus will be there. Bello, you will plant the harmonic charges at the cardinal points of the chamber. Chioma, monitor the structural integrity. The moment we introduce the counter-frequency, the environment may become… volatile.”

“And me, Colonel?” Eze asked.

“You will listen. For the flaw in the copy. For the break in the song.”

“And you?”

Adeshina hefted the silver emitter. “I will play the instrument.”

The plan was set. A plan of silence, of precision, of using the enemy’s own stolen face against it. It was a good plan. A professional plan. It felt, to Adeshina, like arranging deck chairs on a ship already swallowed by the abyss. He looked at their faces, these quiet specialists, and saw the faith they placed in his command, in the legend of the Leopard. He was about to lead them into a darkness that wore his own skin, wielding a light that would sear his soul. The corruption was not waiting at Isale Station. It was sitting at this table, giving orders.

---

**Scene 2**

The entrance to the overflow channel was a mouth of weeping stone, hidden behind a cascade of thorny bougainvillea at the edge of a derelict park. The air here was different. It was thick, soupy, pressing on the eardrums. The sounds of the city—the distant sirens, the hum of generators—were gone, replaced by a low, sub-auditory thrum that vibrated in the teeth. It was the sound of the world holding its breath.

They went in single file, wading into black, stagnant water that came to their thighs. The light from their helmet lamps did not beam; it *coagulated*, forming short, greasy yellow cones that seemed to push against the darkness rather than illuminate it. The walls of the brick tunnel were slick with a strange, non-reflective moss that drank the light. Their splashing footsteps echoed, but wrongly—the sound came back flattened, truncated, as if the echoes were being consumed a few feet away.

Chioma led, her eyes on a handheld scanner. “The water is getting colder. Fourteen degrees. The ambient temperature is twenty-two. The heat is being siphoned. The laws are… bending.”

Adeshina felt it. The glacial river in his spine was now in concert with the water around him. The cold was not an absence of heat, but an active, sucking presence. He held the silver emitter before him like a dowsing rod. It was inert, waiting.

They moved deeper. The tunnel began to change. The brickwork, once perfectly laid, now showed hairline fractures that pulsed with a faint, rhythmic blackness, like veins pumping ink. The air grew heavier, tasting of ozone and damp soil and something else—a metallic tang, like blood on the tongue. The thrumming deepened, resolving into a sound. It was a bass note, a vibration that seemed to originate in the bones of the earth. And beneath it, almost subliminal, a melody. A twisted, atonal rendition of a military cadence. *His* cadence. The one he used to march his first battalion.

Eze, behind him, whispered, “It is learning. It is moving from signature to song. It is building a mythology.”

Ahead, Chioma stopped. “The conduit opens into the main access shaft. Fifty-meter vertical descent. The ladder is… compromised.”

They gathered at the edge. The shaft was a cylinder of pure black. Their lights revealed the first ten meters of an iron-rung ladder. The rungs were there, but their surfaces seemed to writhe, coated in the same light-eating moss. Below, nothing. Not darkness, but nullity.

Bello uncoiled a syntherope, testing its anchor. “I will go first. The charges need stable placement.”

He descended, swallowed by the black. They waited. The only sound was the drip of water from their clothes and the oppressive, singing dark. Adeshina’s comm crackled.

“Bello. Report.”
